Abstract
Distinguishing neurocognitive disorders (NCD) can be difficult due to similarities in their symp-
toms, but is important to insure correct treatment. Graph theory is a useful tool for characteris-
ing complex networks, and has previously been applied in conjunction with functional imaging.
The functional imaging captures temporal and spatial patterns of neuronal communication be-
tween brain regions, which is then mapped to graph nodes and edges representing the brain
regions and communication between them respectively.
Although recent papers have attempted to utilize graph theory to characterize brain network
dysfunction in order to classify NCDs, a large scale evaluation of relevance of the different graph
theory metrics has yet to be identified.
This thesis seeks to examine the utility of various graph theory metrics for classifying NCDs,
by focusing on the task of discriminating between 16 healthy controls and 15 patients with
Parkinson’s disease (PD) based on global (graph), regional and local (node) characterization of
the brain network.
Changes in brain network characteristics of PD patients compared to healthy age matched
controls were evaluated using Imaginary Coherence (ImCoh) and as a measure of connectivity
between individual electrode placements during resting-state electroencephalography (EEG).
Application of thresholding is nontrivial and has high impact on the ability to discern net-
work differences between the two groups. Therefore, optimization of graph thresholding was
implemented to increase sparsity of the graphs and remove weaker connections, using both
weight-based and density-based methods. Optimal thresholds often differed across frequency
bands and spatial level (global, regional and local graph metrics).
The graph metrics were used for classification using Logistic Regression (LogReg) and Support
Vector Classifiers (SVC), from which the most informative graph features were the node degree,
closeness vitality and effective size. The three graph metrics reflect the integration, importance
and redundancy of a node in the graph.
The LogReg with density-based thresholding achieved an average f1-score of 0.726 with only
36 features, all of which were node degree, closeness vitality and effective size features. This
illustrates the potential capabilities of these three feature types for classification of NCDs in
future studies, yet the results are based on a limited PD data set, with the models’ ability to
generalize being unknown and could therefore be overfitted to the data. Additionally the degree
of cognitive impairment could either be classified using multi-class or ideally using regression to
better capture the continuous spectrum of cognitive impairment. Further work must be done to
sufficiently evaluate the potential of graph theory for classifying various NCDs, yet this thesis
provides preliminary results that suggests its promising application for diagnostics of PD.

Resum´e
Differentiering af neurokognitive lidelser kan besværliggøres af overlappende symptomer, men
er vigtig for at sikre korrekt behandling. Graf teori er et nyttigt værktøj til karakterisering af
komplekse netværker. Det har tidligere været brugt i samspil med funktionel billeddannelse,
som opfanger b˚ade tids- og lokations aspekter af neuronale kommunikations mønstre mellem
forskellige dele af hjernen, hvilket overføres til graf noder og kanter der repræsenterer henholdsvis
hjernens regioner og kommunikationen imellem dem.
Selvom artikler for nyligt har forsøgt at benytte graf teori til karakterisering af dysfunktion af
hjernens netværk i forsøg p˚a at klassificere neurokognitive lidelser, mangler der en overordnet
evaluering af relevansen af de forskellige graf teori metrikker.
Denne afhandling forsøger at undersøge brugbarheden af diverse graf teori metrikker brugt til
klassificering af neurokognitive lidelser, ved at fokusere p˚a differentiering af 16 raske kontroller
og 15 patienter med Parkinson’s sygdom baseret p˚a global (graf), regional og lokal (node) karak-
terisering af hjernens netværk.
Strukturændringer af hjernens netværk for Parkinson’s patienter sammenlignet med alders
matchet kontroller blev evalueret ved brug af Imaginary Coherence (ImCoh) som m˚al for kon-
nektivitet mellem individuelle elektrode placeringer for elektroencefalografi (EEG) under hvile.
Brugen af thresholding er ikke triviel og har en stor indflydelse p˚a evnen til at adskille netværks
forskelle mellem to forskellige grupper. Derfor blev optimering af thresholding implementeret
ved brug af vægt-baserede og densitets-baserede metoder, hvilket forenkler grafen og fjerne
svage forbindelser. Optimale thresholds varierede ofte mellem frekvensb˚and og niveau (globale,
regionale og lokale graf metrikker).
Graf metrikkerne blev brugt til klassificering ved brug af logistisk regression (LogReg) og Sup-
port Vector Classifiers (SVC), for hvilke de mest informative graf metrikker var node degree,
closeness vitality og effective size.
De tre grafmetrikker reflekterer integration, vigtighed og
redundans af en node i grafen.
Logistisk regression med densitets-baseret thresholding opn˚aede en gennemsnitlig f1-score p˚a
0.726 med kun 36 metrikker, hvoraf alle metrikkerne var enten node degree, closeness vitality
eller effective size. Dette illustrerer disse metrikkers potentielle evner til i fremtidige studier
at klassificere neurokognitive lidelser. Dog er resultaterne baseret p˚a et begrænset Parkinson’s
data sæt, hvor modellens evne til at generalisere er uvis og kunne derfor være overfittet til data.
Graden af kognitiv svækkelse kunne yderligere enten klassificeres ved brug af flere klasser eller
ideelt set ved brug af regression til at fange det kontinuerlige spektrum af kognitiv svækkelse.
Videre arbejde m˚a udføres for tilstrækkeligt at kunne evaluere potentialet for graf teori til
klassificering af forskellige neurokognitive lidelser. Denne afhandling indledende resultater der
indikerer at brugen af graf teori skulle være lovende inden for diagnostik af Parkinson’s sygdom.

1
Introduction
It is estimated that around 14 % of all people older than 72 have a form of Neurocognitive
disorder (NCD), more commonly referred to as dementia. An NCD is characterized by impair-
ment of daily life activities differentiated from those expected during normal aging. Currently,
researchers are targeting biomarkers to detect early onset or for aiding in evaluation of possible
treatments. The different types of NCDs can have similar symptoms and neurophysiological
findings, though the underlying cause, and therefore treatments, varies.
The focus of this thesis is on characterizing Parkinson’s Disease (PD), which over time can lead
to the development of an NCD. PD shares many characteristics of pathology with that of the
most abundant cause of NCD, namely Alzheimer’s Disease (AD), one of them being that they
both can lead to brain network dysfunction. [15] To better understand the difference between
the networks of the healthy vs. diseased human brain, its internal connections measured using
Electroencephalogram (EEG) can be simplified and visualized using graph theory. The first use
of graph theory on EEG data was on AD patients in 2007 and continues to be used today to
investigate the usages of graph theory to characterize different types of neurological diseases.
Recently, structural brain networks have gained the attention of the neuroscientific community
for its link to cognitive function.
This thesis sought to examine the importance of different graph theory features for classify-
ing PD and healthy control (HC) subjects using a variety of graph metrics derived from the
Imaginary Coherence (ImCoh) between different regions of the brain during a resting-state
Electroencephalogram (rsEEG).
Based on graph theory, it might be possible to not only characterize the differences in network
structure of the HC and NCD subjects, but also aid in distinguishing various types of NCDs
based on their underlying cause. Additionally, it could aid in quantifying the progression of
the cognitive impairment, as some of the currently used methods like the Mini-Mental State
Examination (MMSE) struggle to capture changes in cognition during the early stages.

2
Theory
2.1
The Human Brain
Anatomy and common labeling of the brain can be seen in Figure 1. A deep fissure splits the
brain into two halves, namely the left and right hemisphere, which communicate through nerve
fibers located at the base of the fissure.[54]
Figure 1: Sagittal plane of brain lobes and their primary function. Modified image from [85].
The functional part of the brain consists mainly of networks of neurons, which serve to distribute
the activity of the brain such that information is shared and processed across different regions.
The communication between neurons is mediated through chemically induced electrical poten-
tials. The neuronal networks of the brain operate at both a spatial and temporal level and some
neurological diseases can affect the functioning of these neuronal networks. In order to have an
exchange of information from one brain region to another there must exist an anatomical struc-
ture that connects the two regions. Cognitive functions rely on the flexibility and adaptability
of the brain networks in order to make fast changes based on the change in cognitive tasks.[78]
Reconstruction of the anatomical structures happen on a larger timescale, therefore Pascal Fries
[29] proposed a hypothesis which enables fast dynamical changes, namely the ’communication-
through-coherence’ hypothesis. It is based on the idea that the phase locking (synchronization
of the phase of their oscillations) of two groups of neurons determines the ease by which the
two can communicate, since neuronal groups that are in phase will be excitable at the same
time. Pascal Fries describes this as the communication windows for input and output for the
two neuronal groups being open at the same time.[29] Oscillatory synchronization thereby serves
as the driving factor for construction of larger networks and propagation of information from
one neural population to another.[20]
2.2
Electroencephalography (EEG)
By placing electrodes on the scalp, neural activity can be assessed through measuring of the
voltage fluctuations. The measured EEG signals are defined as the change in electrical potential
between the measurement electrode and the reference electrode provided in units of micro volt.
The oscillations of the neural populations within the brain are reflected in the measured EEG
signals. EEG has a high temporal resolution enabling the measuring of cognitive processes in
the brain.[20] On the other hand it has a poorer spatial resolution limited by the number of and
placement of electrodes along with the greater distance between the neurons inside the brain
and electrodes on the scalp. What is measured by the electrodes is the activity of neural sources
residing within the brain linearly projected onto the scalp. An electrode thereby measures the
2 of 135
==PAGE:11==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
joined activity of multiple sources, each located internally in the brain at a certain distance from
the electrode. Activity from the one neural source can therefore be picked up by two or more
neighbouring electrodes via projection or simply by propagation of the signal on the scalp. This
is referred to as volume conduction and is most problematic when dealing with the scalp EEG
recordings as opposed to e.g. intracranial electroencephalography (iEEG).
The signals are initially in what is referred to as the sensor-space, since the measured signals
reflect the projection of source activity onto the scalp. The EEG signals can be transformed
into source-space as a weighted sum of all measured signals, which reflects an estimation of the
underlying activity at selected locations within the brain, with increasing number of electrodes
improving the interpolation of the source activity.[46] The mapping from sensor to source-space
requires assumptions regarding the head shape, brain shape and conductivity of the tissues.
EEG can be used to measure brain activity when performing a task or during rest which is
referred to as resting state (rs). The EEG signals are easily affected by electrical noise from
the power lines and EEG artifacts such as eye-movement, head muscle activation, electrode
displacement and electrical interference.[46] The brain activity is often examined within certain
frequency bands that reflect different functions of the brain, as described in Table 1. The limits
of are not standardized, leading papers to often use slightly different ranges of frequency bands,
as evident in Appendix A.8 Tables 29 and 30. The ranges defined in Table 1 are the ones used
for the entirety of this thesis.
Frequency band
Range
Physiological and cognitive function
δ
Delta-band
1-4 Hz
Deep sleep and repairing [18]
θ
Theta-band
4-8 Hz
Memory and cognitive control [20]
α
Alpha-band
8-12 Hz
Present during eyes closed (ec) and resting state (rs) [58]
β
Beta-band
12-30 Hz
Focusing, thinking, perception and sustained attention [18]
γ
Gamma-band
30-45 Hz
Learning, extreme focus and high cognitive processing [18]
Table 1: Range of frequency bands and some of the brain functions they cover.
During rsEEG aging is associated with a global increase in power of the lower frequencies and a
decrease in activity in the alpha band.[35] Age matched HC are therefore necessary in order to
correctly characterize the affected brain function associated with a given neurological disease.
The signals measured during and EEG can help identify alterations in brain functioning of mul-
tiple neurological diseases. Some diseases have fewer and hard to discern symptoms at onset
and diagnostic tools like imaging and EEG can aid in diagnosing some of these diseases earlier.
Other neurological diseases like dementia might rely mainly on evaluation of performance of
daily tasks, observable symptoms and cognitive tests.
A common tools for grading the global cognitive function of a patient is the MMSE, which attends
to assessment of orientation, registration, attention and calculation, recall and language. The
examination consists of questions and tasks, each correct response equals 1 point with a total of
30 point possible. The general grading of MMSE scores in terms of cognitive function can aid
in classifying patients as either Cognitively Normal (CN) or having Mild Cognitive Impairment
(MCI), Moderate Cognitive Impairment or Severe Cognitive Impairment (SCI). The MMSE score
are to be expected to decrease with age and increase with educational level.[66] The cognitive
function is a continuous spectrum and classification is used to group people of similar level of
impairment and possibly similar symptoms or lack thereof. The MMSE score has limitations
when it comes to diagnosing MCI, hence other cognitive assessment methods might be of more
use if MCI is suspected.[43]

2.3
Neurocognitive disorder (NCD)
NCD, normally referred to as dementia, is a mental disorder that affects the cognitive functions
of the patient including thinking and memory.[53] There exists different types of dementia,
Alzheimer’s type dementia being the most abundant. The common types of dementia includes:
[17, 6]
• Alzherimer’s Disease Dementia: caused by accumulation of tau-tangles inside the
neurons and beta-amyloid protein buildup outside the neurons, it initially affects mainly
the short term memory.
Symptoms will progressively worsen over time, and includes
confusion, disorientation among others. Accounts for ∼60-80% of all cases of dementia.
• Cerebroascular Dementia: connected to strokes or other types of compromised blood
flow to areas of the brain. Symptoms depend on the affected brain region and will abruptly
worsen as new cases of strokes occur. Accounts for ∼5-10% of all cases of dementia.
• Dementia with Lewy Bodies: is the accumulation of alpha-synuclein in the neurons of
the brain, resulting in symptoms similar to that of Alzheimer’s Disease like memory loss
and confusion, whilst also having sleep pattern disturbances. Parkinsonian movement and
balance problems can also occur.
• Fronto-temporal Dementia: is characterized by atrophy of the frontal and temporal
lobes affecting behavior and personality. Unlike Alzheimer’s Disease the memory is not
affected in the early stages of the disease.
• Parkinson’s Disease Dementia: is the accumulation of alpha-synuclein in substantia
nigra, thought to degrade the dopamine producing nerve cells. Dementia is thought to
manifest when the alpha-synuclein accumulates in the cortex (similar to Dementia with
Lewy Bodies) or beta-amyloid and tau-tangles accumulation (similar to that of Alzheimer’s
Dementia).
• Mixed Dementia: the presence of more than one type of dementia. Resulting symptoms
will be a mixture of the underlying dementia causes.
• Reversible dementia: could be due to reasons such as high brain pressure, vitamin
deficiency, thyroid hormone imbalance or medically induced.
Even though there are many underlying causes of dementia, common for them all is the resulting
affected cognitive function often graded by severity into mild cognitive impairment (MCI), mod-
erate cognitive impairment and severe cognitive impairment (SCI). Distinction between different
types of NCDs is often difficult due to overlapping symptoms.[8]
Parkinson’s Disease
PD is a chronic progressive neurological degenerative disorder characterized by a lack of dopamin-
ergic neurons in the basal ganglia, which functions by inhibiting unnecessary movements. This
is important for starting, stopping and monitoring movement of the body. PD is a hypokinetic
disease of diminished motor activity, but apart from motor symptoms around 20 % of PD pa-
tients will at a later stage develop severe dementia with an average onset around 10 years after
the PD diagnosis [7], though with a slower rate of decline as apposed to AD. The cognitive dys-
function of PD patients often affect frontal lobe executive function (initiating and performing
tasks), memory retrieval and visuospatial discrimination.[27]
PD patients have been found to have dysfunction of the Default Mode Network, which is char-
acterized by an increased basal activity during rest and decreased activity during cognitive
tasks.[25, 69] Other neurological findings for PD are presented in Appendix A.8 Table 28.

2.4
Brain Signals and Connectivity
Brain connectivity refers to the relationship between two or more regions of the brain. Bivariate
connectivity is a measure of activity between only two brain sites as opposed to multivariate
connectivity. Bivariate connectivity has the advantage of simplicity and is more easily imple-
mented and interpreted, yet has the added disadvantage of possible inflation of estimates of
connectivity if the underlying network is actually multivariate. Being that the brain is in fact
highly multivariate, bivariate connectivity measures might suggest a direct connection between
two areas even if the measured connectivity is based on a third shared connection.[20]
The connectivity can be calculated as a measure of correlation, which would be referred to
as functional connectivity, while connectivity based on causality is referred to as effective
connectivity.[20] Throughout this thesis EEG based functional connectivity will be used, and
will therefore for simplicity be referred to simply as “connectivity”.
Different methods for calculating connectivity exists, most of them being based on either the
power or phase of the measured signals.
The two methods reflect different aspects of the
physiological dynamics of the brain, as power reflects the amount of neurons that are active
within the neural population while phase reflects the temporal timing of activity within a neural
population.[46] The phase based connectivity measures at a given frequency are based on the
phase differences between two given signals, for EEG provided by two different electrodes or
source locations. It is based on the principle that functionally coupled neural populations are
phase synchronized in their oscillations. Under the assumption that there is no time-lag between
the measured electrical potential at the scalp and the source activity, phase-based connectivity
has the advantage that it has the ability to discern zero-phase difference components which
can be caused by instantaneous spurious interactions of volume conduction. The power based
connectivity is based on the correlation of time-frequency power of two given signals, for which
the time and frequency is not necessarily the same for both signals.[20]
Brain connectivity can therefore be used to map out the different aspects of the brain network
to asses network disruptions related to neurological diseases.[78] Given the various connectivity
measures, the choice of connectivity method should be based on the specific use case, as no
method is suitable for all possible intended purposes of brain network assessment. The relation
between the three connectivity measures coherency, coherence and imaginary coherence is cov-
ered in the remainder of this section, yet many other applicable connectivity measures exists.
In order to clarify the differences between the three connectivity measures an example is pro-
vided for the case of two measures signals xi(t) and xj(t).
Given the signal xi(t) its Fourier Transform is given as:[67]

In Figure 3 is shown visualizations of the effect of time averaging the cross-spectra for three
different cases of (a) zero phase difference, (b) constant phase difference and (c) random phase
differences. It has previously been stated that the angle of the arrow is dependent on the phase
difference between the two signal, which is also the case for time averaging of the cross-spectra
if the phase difference is constant across the time segments.
For the case of random phase
difference the changing phase difference between time segments ultimately causes the angles of
the individual arrows of the cross-spectra for each time segment, when averaged over time, to go
towards zero. Therefore the time averaged cross-spectrum arrow of the random phase differences
in Figure 3c has a length of 0, as opposed to the two cases of constant phase in Figures 3a and 3b
where the length of the time averaged cross-spectrum arrow is equal to the average length of
the cross-spectra arrows for each of the time segments. The time averaging is applied such that
only phase differences that are non-random are preserved, based on the idea that two neural
populations that oscillate at a constant phase difference are functionally coupled such that the
timing of their oscillatory activity becomes synchronized.[20]

Figure 3: Polar coordinate representation of the effect of time averaging multiple cross-spectra
(cross-PSDs) for different cases of phase differences between the two signals xi(t) and xj(t).
The examples are illustrated at a given frequency f, for a) constant zero phase difference, b)
constant π/2 phase difference and c) non-constant random phase difference. For all three cases
the cross-spectra of the two signals (Sxixj(f)) are depicted in polar coordinates for each of the
four time segments, along with the time averaged cross-spectrum depicted by the blue arrow.
The images are modified reconstructions of Figure 3 from [11].
If there is no phase difference between the two signals averaged over multiple time segments, the
angle of the vector will be 0, similarly if there is a general phase difference between the two signals
averaged over multiple time segments the angle of the vector will be equal to the phase difference
in radians. If there is not a consistent phase difference between the signals across multiple time
segments, then the phase difference will be random for each time segment and will average out
towards zero consequently causing the length (magnitude) of the arrow to converge to zero.[11]
Not applying time averaging would cause all time segment cross-spectra to have a phase and a
non-zero magnitude, from which it is impossible to determine if the measured phase difference
is actually constant over time or if it is simply a single sample of a random phase difference signal.
A neurological example of random phase difference could be measurement of two brain regions
that are not synchronized in their oscillations. Constant non-zero phase difference could indi-
cate synchronization of the brain region and can be assumed to be either directly or indirectly
connected. Constant zero-phase can be seen if the same noise artifact is present in the two elec-
trodes at the same time (zero time-lag) which is the case for artifacts explained by the common
reference problem and volume conduction, which are further explained in Section 2.4.

Due to the limitations of estimating the phase of a signal, the phase will always in radians be
estimated as a value in the range of [0:2π[, as any phase above this would simply be equiva-
lent to taking a given number of trips around the unit circle, while negative phases would be
equivalent of moving in the opposite direction around the unit circle. Phase differences (∆Φ)
on the other hand can take on negative values, now limited to the range of ]-2π:2π[. Based
on this range of possible values for phase difference, the imaginary coherence will be 0 when
the phase differences is equal to either [ -π, 0, π ], assuming that ±2π is not a possible phase
difference value. Since the polar representation of phase is cyclic on the unit circle, actual phase
differences of multiples of ±π would also result in an imaginary coherence of 0. For ∆Φ = 0 the
two signals must have identical phase, while for ∆Φ = −π and ∆Φ = π the two signals have
opposite phase (in terms on phase within [0:2π[ range of the unit circle). The case of ∆Φ = π
is depicted in time segment 3 of Figure 3c, which from a physiological perspective would corre-
spond to two neural populations oscillating completely out of phase. Based on the hypothesis of
’communication-through-coherence’ explained in Section 2.1 the two neural populations would
have minimal overlap of their corresponding input and output windows, thereby minimizing the
ease by which the two populations can communicate.
The imaginary coherence therefore removes the connectivity components that arise from zero
time-lag processes caused by volume conduction and common referencing along with connectivity
of completely out of phase neural populations.
Under the assumption that both the phase
coupling between two brain regions at a certain frequency and the value of the phase difference
reflects the ability or ease by which the two regions can communicate, the utilization of ImCoh
only eliminates components that are assumed to not reflect true brain connectivity.
“True
brain connectivity” being used to describe measured connectivity that reflects an actual direct
connection between the two brain regions through which information can be shared.[57]
Interpretation of Connectivity
The common reference problem occurs when all electrodes share the same reference electrode,
where all of the electrodes therefore reflects the measured activity at the reference electrode.
This would in turn cause spurious correlations between the channels, that does not reflect true
connections within the brain. This can be avoided by selecting a different method of referencing
or by selecting a connectivity measure that omits zero time-lagged components, since the effect
of referencing affects all channels at the same time there will be zero time-lag between the ref-
erencing component of the channels.[11]
The common input problem is where two measured sources are not directly connected but both
receiving a common input from a third source. The common input to the two measured sources
can have the same or different time-lags. For the case of identical time-lags for the two sources
coherence would suggest a neuronal interaction between the two measured sources resulting in
spurious interference from which incorrect conclusions about the connection between the two
sources could be drawn. Using connectivity measures such as imaginary coherence would solve
this problem as it omits all connections between sources that are based on zero time-lag.[11]
Volume conduction can affect the calculated connectivity measures due to two (often neighbour-
ing) electrodes measuring the same electronic signal originating directly from one area of the
brain or by two electrodes measuring the same signal due to propagation of the signal on the
scalp of the subject. The signal originating from one area of the brain is assumed to instanta-
neously be measured at the electrodes, thus having zero time-lag and thereby also zero phase-lag
between the signals measured at the two electrodes. This introduces a high connectivity which
could be a result of either neighbouring electrodes measuring the same signal or true connectivity
of two brain regions, thereby limiting the interpretability of the results.[21]
11 of 135
==PAGE:20==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
The effects of volume conduction can be limited by e.g. application of the inverse method to
estimate the source activity or using specific phase-based connectivity measures that are insen-
sitive to volume conduction artifacts.[57] If the connectivity measure ignores zero phase-lag it
is assumed that contribution of volume conduction is omitted from the data, but at the cost of
also excluding the possible true fast connectivity at zero time delay.[22] From a physiological
perspective the communication between two regions is expected to have a time delay propor-
tional to the length of the path the signal travels, which means that at least non-neighbouring
brain regions are expected to have non-zero time delay.[63]
If two electrodes are affected by the same artifact is might cause the connectivity to be high and
proper artifact removal is therefore important prior to calculating connectivity, if the connec-
tivity is sought to reflect only the brain activity. Another disadvantage of connectivity is that
differences in connectivity values can simply be due to differences in signal-to-noise ratio, and the
impact of noise should be sought decreased both during data acquisition and preprocessing.[11]
2.5
Brain Networks
As depicted in Figure 5 a graph (G) consists of nodes (n) and edges (e) connecting the individual
nodes to each other. A brain network can be represented as a graph or adjacency matrix as
seen in Figure 5g and Figure 5h respectively. The graph form consists of a visual representation
of the nodes and edges, while the adjacency matrix (A) of shape [N,N], where N is the total
number of nodes in the graph, can be used to display the edges of pairwise node combinations.
As can be seen in Figure 5 the graph can be either directed (a,e) or undirected (c,g). For di-
rected graphs the connection between two nodes has directionality indicated by the edges being
drawn as arrows (→), the direction of the arrow indicating the flow of information. An edge
between two nodes (n1 and n2) can therefore e.g. exists when going from n1 to n2 but not when
going from n2 to n1.[26]. Undirected graphs has no directionality thus the edges are drawn as
lines (-) and the adjacency matrix is symmetric around its diagonal. For undirected graphs a
connection between e.g. node 1 (n1) and node 2 (n2) therefore corresponds to both an edge
going from n1 to n2 AND n2 to n1. Undirected graphs can be the result of a lack of causality,
that only a connection between two nodes is observed but the direction of flow of information
is unknown. Directed graphs can therefore offer a more detailed depiction of a network structure.
Also evident from Figure 5 is that a graph can be either weighted (a,c) where all edges have
individual weights or binary (e,g) where all nodes have a weight of either 0 or 1.

==PAGE:22==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
When measuring brain signals using EEG, nodes correspond to the individual electrodes placed
on the scalp (sensor level) or internal locations interpolated from the electrodes (source level).
It can be hard to directly infer anything from visual inspection of the adjacency matrix, which
might be possible for the graph. The graph representation can have an added information about
the spatial positioning of the nodes. For graphs based on EEG measurements the node posi-
tioning corresponds to the EEG electrode layout or interpolated positions thereof.
Neighbouring nodes are defined as two nodes that are directly connected to each other via an
edge. Two nodes (ni and nj) are connected by a path if there exists a sequence of nodes (first
and last node in the sequence being ni and nj respectively) with intermediate edges.[68] The
length of the path between ni and nj is equal to the number of edges on the path, defined as:
|path(ni, nj)|
A neighbour graph Gn(ni) is a subgraph of G defined for a specific node ni. The neighbour
graph of node ni is equal to a subgraph consisting of the neighbouring nodes of node ni (with
or without the inclusion of node ni itself) along with all of the edges between them.
A node is referred to as an articulation point if by removing the node and its associated edges
the graph would become disconnected.[14]
A graph can be subdivided into groups of more densely connected nodes, also referred to as
communities. The communities of a graph can be found using many different algorithms, with
the resulting communities (c) being given as:
c = [c(n1), c(n2), ..., c(nN)]
where each element of the vector is an integer value between 1 and the total number of commu-
nities within the graph. The value of ci representing the community number of the community
containing node i.
An equivalent random graph Gr is constructed by swapping the edges of the original graph G,
whilst preserving the degree of each node. The degree of a node being the number of edges
directly connected to it, see Equation (23) in Appendix A.5.
The equivalent lattice graph Gl is a regular form of a reconstructed graph, where the edges
are swapped whilst keeping the graph from becoming disconnected. The two graph types rep-
resent opposite ends of the spectrum random ↔deterministic, evident for some of the main
graph characterization metrics such as Clustering Coefficient (C(G)) and Average Shortest Path
Length (L(G)). Formulas and descriptions of the two graph metrics can be seen in Appendix A.5
Table 15. For equivalent random and lattice graphs with a sufficiently large number of nodes
and edges, the following applies: [44]
C(Gr) ≪C(Gl)
(13)
L(Gr) ≪L(Gl)
(14)
such that a random graph is characterized by having both low clustering coefficient and low
average shortest path length, the opposite of which applies to the lattice graph.

Graph Thresholding
The idea of thresholding weighted edges of a graph is based on removing the edges that contain
mostly smaller spurious connections, enabling calculation of certain network metrics, emphasis-
ing certain properties of the network topology and lastly reducing the computational cost by
limiting the number of edges in the graph.
When thresholding a graph, often times edges with a weight smaller than a chosen threshold
(th) are set equal to 0 or −1 (depending on the range of the connectivity measure). It can also
be decided to keep the edge weights above the threshold and set the ones below equal to 0, or
any variation thereof. This method is based on the assumption that the edge weights reflects
the importance of the edge, i.e. the greater the weight the higher the importance, which might
not be the case for brain interactions.[39] In order to reduce the number of connections not
reflecting true brain connectivity, as these tend to greatly affect the estimation of some graph
metrics, it has been suggested to remove as many edges as possible.[86]
A higher threshold value would remove the effects of “smaller” spurious connections and result
in graphs containing only the strongest connections. Choosing a high threshold simultaneously
eliminates the weaker connections which are important for network stability and maintaining
connections between functional brain modules or regions. Threshold at lower values can however
also be useful for distinguishing different groups for certain connectivity measures and graph
features.[1] The thresholding process is therefore not trivial and thought should be given to
both the choice of threshold method and value. Thresholding as a processing step for binariz-
ing a weighted graph can in turn be used to enable the calculation of certain network metrics.[78]
To calculate some of the network features and for the extracted features to vary between different
binary graphs, it is sought to threshold the edges such that the graph is neither disconnected nor
fully connected. Fully connected binary graphs result in identical features across multiple graphs
while disconnected graphs prevent the calculation of features like path lengths and clustering
coefficient (see feature formulas and descriptions in Appendix A.5 Table 15 ).[1, 78]
A fully connected node is when a node shares an edge with all other nodes, while a fully
disconnected node is when the node has no edges. Therefore the threshold value (th) must
comply with the following for all nodes, based on edge weights in the adjacency matrix:
N
min
j=1 (Aij) < thi <
N
max
j=1 (Aij)
(15)
where thi is the threshold value for node i, Aij is the weight of the edge between node i and
node j and N is the total number of nodes in the graph. Since a common threshold value is
to be applied to the entire graph, the threshold value must comply with the threshold limits
defined for each node as in Equation (15) if it is sought to avoid both fully connected and fully
disconnected graph. Yet for the purpose of this thesis, it is only sought to avoid the cases of
a fully connected graph and disconnected graph, meaning all nodes can not be fully connected
and no node must be disconnected.
A fully connected graph is when all possible edges are present in the graph. This can be avoided
by simply selecting a graph threshold (thG) value that is higher than the minimum edge value
for all nodes, thereby remove at least one or more edges from the graph. A disconnected graph
is when one or more nodes are not connected to any of the other node in the graph. This can
be avoided by selecting a thG lower than the smallest of the maximum edge values calculated
for each node, such that the node with the lowest maximum edge is not disconnected from the

subject. Thresholding of each frequency band individually is necessary since most average brain
connectivity values are expected to differ between frequency bands.[20]
Many different thresholding techniques exist that are based on either thresholding by a fixed
weight value (weight-based), fixed percentage of edges (density-based), window-thresholding,
distances between nodes, consensus thresholding, standard deviation based or algorithm based
methods like minimum spanning tree (MST). Thresholds can also be applied at either a global
(graph) or local (node) level.[39, 86]
Weight-based thresholding
Weight-based thresholding can be problematic when comparing different subject groups. Firstly,
the subject-to-subject variation might cause there to not exist a threshold value that satisfies
the threshold intervals of all subjects (if min > max). Secondly, the possible differences between
groups in terms of network features might be difficult to distinguish from the more simple dif-
ferences in number of edges in the graphs. Density-based thresholding can therefore be used
to insure that each graph contains the same amount of edges, and thereby all have the same
density (as defined by Equation (22) in Appendix A.5).

Density-based thresholding
The density-based thresholding (also referred to as percentage-based thresholding) technique is
based on having a fixed value of density (see Equation (22) in Appendix A.5), resulting in all
graphs having the same density and therefore also the same total amount of edges (provided
that all graphs have the same number of nodes). One problem with this approach would be that
for fixed density and total number of nodes, some of the network features introduced in Sec-
tion 2.5.1 would be identical across all graphs, rendering these features useless for distinguishing
differences between the graphs at a later stage.[86]

2.5.1
Network Characterization
The topology of a graph can be characterized by different network metrics referred to as fea-
tures, which can be extracted at both node level (local), graph level (global) or for a region or
grouping of nodes. The features reflect different characteristics about the distribution of edges
between the nodes of the graph. An overview of features including their formula and description
is included in Table 15 in Appendix A.5.
Some features can be calculated using weighted edges, and others require a binarized graph. As
previously explained thresholding can be used to binarize a graph by means of either a weight
based or density based approach. Both approaches can cause variations in feature values, and
the threshold value should be selected with this in mind.
Global network features are a generalization of the network and similar feature values does not
necessarily mean similar networks, as structurally different networks can yield the same global
feature values.[1] The graph based features are often calculated as either the sum or the average
of the node based features.
2.6
Classifiers
Logistic Regression
The logistic regression (LogReg) is a linear model based on probabilities, where classification is
acquired by thresholding probabilities at 0.5. The LogReg works by fitting a logistic function
(sigmoidal curve) to the training data. The logistic function is seen in Figure 6 given by:
ˆp(xi, w) = P(y = 1|xi, w) =
1
1 + exp(−xi · w)
where ˆp(xi, w) is the probability of the positive class (y = 1) given the data point xi and weights
w, xi is the data point of subject i, w are the feature weights and y is the output.[72]
Figure 6: Logistic function in one dimension.
For the data points it applies that xi ∈Rn+1, meaning xi is a vector of length n+1, n being the
number of features, with the first element of xi being 1. For the feature weights it applies that
w ∈Rn+1 with the first element of w being w0. The w0 weight affects the center of the function

==PAGE:27==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
while the remaining feature weights determines the slope of the logistic function in each of the
n dimensions, where large weights results in steeper slopes.
When fitting a LogReg the following optimization problem seeks to minimize the cost function:

where w are the feature weights, C is the regularization parameter (the inverse of the reg-
ularization strength) which for high values means that the log likelihood is penalised higher
than then feature weights in order to minimize the cost function. Lower values of C therefor
results in a stronger regularization, while the opposite is also true.[72] As regularization is ap-
plied to avoid over-fitting and increase generalization, lower values of C can lead to under-fitting.
The regularization term r(w) can be set to either ’L1’, ’L2’, the combination of ’L1’ and ’L2’
(ElasicNet) or entirely removed, where L1 is the l1-norm = absolute value of the weight (L1 =
||w||1) and L2 is the l2-norm squared = the weight squared (L2 = 1

2).[72]
Support Vector Classifier
A Support Vector Machine (SVM) is a method that linearly combines features for solving either
a regression or classification problem. For classification a Support Vector Classifier (SVC) is used
to construct a hyper-plane (decision boundary) in a multi-dimensional space. The SVC seeks
to fit the hyper-plane to the training data such that data points are correctly classified while
the distance from the hyper-plane to the nearest data points is maximized. The distance from
hyper-plane to nearest data point is referred to as the margin, and any data points lying within
or on the margin boundaries are considered support vectors. When the margin is maximized
the ability of the model to generalize is often increased.[71]
Figure 7: SVC decision boundary in two dimensions. Figure inspired by Figure 7.3 of [12].
19 of 135
==PAGE:28==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
SVC seeks to optimize the following constrained cost function:
min
w,b,ξ
1
2||w||2
2 + C
n
X
i=1
ξi

yi(wT ϕ(xi) + b) ≥1 −ξi,
ξi ≥0,
i = 1, ..., n
where w are the weights (w ∈Rp), b is the intercept, n is the number of training data points, C
is the regularization parameter, ξi is the slack parameter and sign(wT ϕ(x) + b) is the prediction
of the label based on the sign (+/-). It is sought to maximize the width of the margin, defined
as
2
||w||2 , which is equal to minimizing 1
2||w||2
2, where ||w||2
2 is the squared l2-norm.[71]
The slack parameter (ξi) is the distance to margins function for which it applies that:
ξi = 0
for correctly classified data points outside or on the margin
0 < ξi < 1
for correctly classified data points inside the margin and on
the right side of the decision boundary
ξi = 1
for data points on the decision boundary
ξ > 1
for incorrectly classified data points
The addition of the slack parameter transforms the SVM from a hard margin to a soft margin,
where the optimization problem can be solved under the constraints when the two data distribu-
tions are not fully linearly separable. For any data point not positioned on the decision boundary
or correctly classified outside the margin the slack parameter is defined as ξi = |ti−y(xi)|, where
ti is the target value.[12]
Higher values of C therefore causes the misclassified data points to be more heavily penalized
in the cost function, resulting in a smaller margin. Higher values of C makes the model more
sensitive to outliers.
Two of the possible kernels (K(x, x′)) for an SVC are defined as:[71]
Linear
’linear’
K(x, x′) = −1 + w
Radial basis function
’rbf’
K(x, x′) = exp(−γ||x −x′||2)
where γ is the kernel coefficient determining the amount of influence the individual training data
points has, given that it determines the width of the ’rbf’ kernel. The kernels can transform the
data into higher order feature space where the data might be more easily linearly separable.[71]

3
Methods
3.1
Parkinson’s Disease data set
Data was obtained from the OpenNeuro database (accession number ds002778), hereafter re-
ferred to as the San Diego data set.[70]
The San Diego data set is comprised of eyes-open resting-state EEG data from 31 subjects (15
PD patients and 16 age-matched controls HC) - prior to releasing the EEG data the authors
removed one PD subject due to the presence of facial dyskinesia (involuntary movements of the
face) during the EEG measurement. PD subjects were classified as having mild to moderate PD
with stages II to III on the Hoehn and Yahr scale.[30] Data includes EEG measurements from 32
channels placed according to Figure 8. Signals were recorded during resting state for a duration
of minimum 3 minutes for each subject with a sampling frequency of 512 Hz. Data was collected
using a ActiveTwo Biosemi Instrumentation system with the left and right mastoids as reference.
Figure 8: Electrode placement of BioSemi 32 channel EEG device used for data collection of the
SanDiego dataset, with colored regions as defined by Aljalal et al. 2022 [4].
The subjects were instructed to relax and focus their gaze on a white cross on a screen. For the
PD subjects data was acquired for both on- and off-medication, yet only off-medication EEG
data will be used in this project. For the off-medication measurements the minimum number of
hours since last dose of medication was 12 hours. Additional meta data of medications, doses
and time between doses can be acquired from the paper by George et al. 2013 [30] that is
based on a more extensive version of the SanDiego data set including measurements during a
stop-signal task. The provided data was not entirely in compliance with the standard of Brain
Imaging Data Structure (BIDS).[62] Basic statistics of the metadata for the PD and HC subjects
of the San Diego data set can be seen in Table 2.

3.2
Signal processing
The signal processing pipeline is depicted in Figure 9, the corresponding processing scripts can be
found Appendix A.9. For reproducibility purposes the random state seed was for all applicable
processing steps set equal to 8.
Figure 9: Signal processing pipeline. The dark blue boxes represents individual Python scripts
which can be found in Appendices A.9.1 to A.9.5.
The following pages contain detailed descriptions of each processing step depicted in Figure 9.
22 of 135
==PAGE:31==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
Bidsify
BIDS conversion
The SanDiego dataset was not entirely BIDS compliant and BIDS conversion was therefore
initially applied to the source data, using the bidsify.py script in Appendix A.9.1. The slightly
modified data is subsequently referred to as raw data.
Preprocessing and artifact removal
Prior to calculation of connectivity the data must be cleaned as much as possible through noise
reduction. This was done to decrease the possible influence of noise on the estimation of brain
connectivity, since some artifacts and noise can produce high connectivity values that does not
reflect true brain connections. All preprocessing was performed using the preprocessing.py script
in Appendix A.9.3.
Remove extra EEG channels
Apart from the regular 32 channels of the SanDiego dataset 8 extra channels and a status chan-
nel exists in the dataset, which were removed prior to filtering of the signals. The excluded extra
channels are: [ ’EXG1’, ’EXG2’, ’EXG3’, ’EXG4’, ’EXG5’, ’EXG6’, ’EXG7’, ’EXG8’, ’Status’ ]
Most commonly occurring non-brain EEG artifacts include eye-blinks, eye-movement, head
muscle activation, electrode displacement and 50/60 Hz electrical interference from the power
lines.[46] Some artifact like power line noise is consistent throughout the entire EEG signal at
a fixed frequency, making removal of this particular artifact a fast and easy process. Other
artifacts require more complicated methods.
Band-pass filter
Removal of power line artifacts is not necessary for the current processing pipeline, as only
frequencies from 1 to 45 Hz will be further processed. If higher frequency bands were to be
included in future studies it would require applying e.g. a notch filter centered at 60 Hz. To
remove any baseline wandering or low frequency drifts all channels are band pass filtered using
an FIR hamming window with a lower pass-band edge of 1 Hz and an upper pass-band edge
of 45 Hz since for the current application only frequencies up until the gamma band are to be
evaluated further.
The manual removing of non-constant artifacts with varying frequencies can be time consuming
when dealing with larger data sets, and requires high level of knowledge regarding EEG and
artifacts types while also being a quite subjective process. Depending on the quality of the EEG
data, manual removal of artifacts might result in loss of large amounts of data. Often times the
frequency range of the artifacts coincide with the frequencies of interest in the later analysis,
preventing the use of more simple filtering solutions.
Visual inspection of EEG and ICA
Visual inspection of the EEG signals in the time domain, is used to identify noise filled channels
(due to e.g. electrode displacement) and time segments (due to e.g. eye-blinks or head move-
ments). In order to avoid the loss of data from removal of all affected segments and channels,
periodically recurring higher amplitude noise elements like eye-blinks can be “subtracted” from
the signal, thereby preserving a large amount of data.
Remove eye-blinks
The semi-automated artifact removal technique Independent Component Analysis (ICA) is used
due to its speed and slightly more objective means of removing artifacts. ICA is used to split
the EEG signal into separate statistically independent components. Components that contain
23 of 135
==PAGE:32==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
non-neural artifacts such as eye-blinks can subsequently be subtracted from the corresponding
channel. Performing ICA therefore still requires knowledge about the shape of the artifacts in
order to remove the correct components. For eye-blink artifact identification the raw filtered
data over time is inspected along with inspection of ICA components over time and topological
distribution of component weights of the individual channels.[20] An example of the plots used
for visual inspection can be seen in Figure 20.
The artifact removal process is especially important when calculating brain connectivity, as ar-
tifacts that affects more than a single electrode could result in higher estimates of connectivity.
This complicates the interpretation of the result, since not all estimated connections would be
due to true brain interactions. Additionally artifact removal also aids in increasing the signal-
to-noise ratio (SNR), which enables the analysis of useful brain signals that would otherwise be
“hidden” in the higher amplitude noise.[11]
Interpolate bad EEG channels
It is sought to keep the number of channels the same for each subject, which poses a problem
when some of the EEG data contains bad channels. An example of a noisy channel can be seen
in Figure 18. One simple method would be that when a bad channel was identified for a specific
subject, that channel was subsequently removed for all other subjects. This insures that all
subjects have the same channels, but it also means a lot of otherwise clean and useful data is
discarded. This also poses the problem that for large data sets it is possible that all channels
would have to be removed, thereby prohibiting any further analysis. Another method would
be to interpolate the missing channel based on the remaining “good” channels.[20] It should be
noted that this could be cause for concern if other connectivity measures were to be calculated
that are not insensitive to zero time-lag components. This is due to fact that interpolation of
a ’bad’ channel is a weighted summation of the signals from the surrounding ’good’ channels,
with zero time-lag. This would increase the connectivity values of the neighbouring nodes at
zero time-lag (zero phase difference).
Since the connectivity measure chosen in this thesis enables the use of this method, the ’bad’
channels were interpolated using the spherical splines method.[51]
Annotate bad EEG time segments
Based on visual inspection the noisy time segments were annotated as ’bad’, meaning that these
annotated segments will not pass the later segmentation step as all segments containing data
annotated as ’bad’ are excluded from further processing. An example of a noisy time segment
can be seen in Figure 19.
Annotate bad beginning and end of EEG data
Events of start and stop of data collection was provided for some of the subjects, and everything
prior to the start-event and after the stop-event were subsequently annotated as ’bad’. If start
and stop events were not provided for a subject they were chosen to default to 1s being anno-
tated as ’bad’ in the beginning and end of the EEG data. This was done to account for possible
disturbances in the beginning and end of the EEG measuring.
The excluded components for each subject along with description of exclusion criteria can be
seen in Appendix A.2. The mapping of which ICA components, EEG channels and EEG time
segments to be removed from each subject is seen in Table 13 of Appendix A.2. The removal
of these elements are done to acquire a higher SNR prior to connectivity calculations, as is it
sought to calculate the connectivity based mainly on recordings of true brain activity as opposed
to muscle activity or other general artifacts.[11]
24 of 135
==PAGE:33==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
Connectivity
Connectivity calculations were performed using the connectivity.py script in Appendix A.9.4,
which outputs adjacency matrices for each epoch, frequency band and subject.
Frequency bands
The EEG data was split into five frequency bands based on Table 1 along with one additional
frequency band spanning all of the frequencies in the other bands. All filtering was performed
using FIR filters.
Epoching
The EEG data for each frequency band is divided into 60 s epochs by splitting the EEG signal
into non-overlapping consecutive epochs across all channels. Since the total duration of EEG
sampling for the SanDiego dataset was minimum 3 minutes for each subject, each subject would
have a total of 3 epochs where any additional epochs were excluded.
Segmentation
Each epoch within each frequency band is further split into 5 s non-overlapping segments across
all channels. A maximum of 12 segments can be extracted from each epoch, with any segment
containing data annotated as ’bad’ being excluded from further processing. The rejected seg-
ments for each subject is listed in Table 14 of Appendix A.2.
Connectivity calculations
Connectivity is calculated using imaginary coherence (ImCoh) as a measure of connectivity be-
tween all pairs of nodes for each subject, epoch and frequency band, using the connectivity.py
script in Appendix A.9.4. The ImCoh is used for EEG connectivity calculations since volume
conduction artifacts do not affect the imaginary part of coherency, assuming that there is no
time-lag between the source activity and the electrical potential measured at the scalp. It is
therefore assumed that ImCoh reflects true underlying interactions between brain regions.[57]
The implemented MNE function [50] is based on the assumptions that each epoch is an in-
dependent sample of the same connectivity network and that within each epoch the spectral
components are stationary. The calculated connectivity measure of each epoch should not be
assessed as a function of time and connectivity is therefore calculated as an average over multiple
time segments.[52]
The resulting data is a connectivity matrix of size [N × N] for each of the three epochs for each
subject, where N is the number of EEG channels.
Feature extraction
Graph conversion
Each of the connectivity matrices are converted to an un-directed graph by using the connectivity
matrix as the adjacency matrix of the graph.
Graph thresholding
Both weight-based and density-based thresholding was performed on the extracted graphs, based
on the theory explained in Section 2.5. Since some of the features to be calculated are depen-
dent on all nodes being connected to the graph, the function find min max threshold was
constructed (see util.py in Appendix A.9.7). It finds both the weight-based and density-based
minimum and maximum thresholds for each frequency band, given that the graph should not
be disconnected nor fully connected for all subjects.
Based on the possible threshold ranges, thresholds (th) were selected at increments of 0.01 for
weight-based and 0.1 for density-based such that: minimum ≤th ≤maximum.

The threshold range and tested thresholds can be seen in Table 3 for the weight-based and
density-based thresholding. The same thresholds for each frequency band were applied to all
subjects, such that any edge weight below the threshold is set equal to 0 while all edge weights
above the threshold are set equal to 1, resulting in binarized graphs.
In order to select the optimal threshold value for each frequency band, all combinations of weight-
based or density-based thresholds for the six frequency bands were tested based on the CV setup
in Figure 10. The f1-score of each inner fold was evaluated based on only the logistic regression
(LogReg, C=1) classifier trained on the inner train set (Dtrain(inner)) and tested on the inner
test set (Dtest(inner)). The average f1-score across all inner folds was acquired for all outer folds,
such that the average f1-score could be calculated across the outer folds. The resulting f1-scores
are seen in Table 7 and Table 9. In order to initially investigate any performance related effects
of graph-based, node-based and region-based features, the f1-score was also evaluated for each
of the feature methods separately. The combination of all features is also tested, to capture
coupling between features to a greater extent. The results of the optimization of threshold value
for each frequency band individually are included in Appendix A.6 Tables 18 to 25.
As classification is to be performed on all features and frequency bands simultaneously, the
threshold values used in the following processing steps are chosen as the combination of thresh-
olds across all frequency bands with the highest f1-score, as presented in Table 7 and Table 9.

Feature calculation
For each subject a feature vector was constructed for each 1 minute epoch. The feature vector
includes features calculated at different spatial levels, namely graph, node and region level for
each frequency band. The types of features are listed below, for which the description and for-
mula can be found in Table 15 of Appendix A.5. The total number of features per graph equals
2.292, per the calculations shown in Appendix A.4.
All features are numerical and the possible ranges of values are included in Appendix A.5 Table 15
for most features. Since a majority of the features have a range of [0 : 1] or [0 : N −1], N being
the total number of nodes in the graph, scaling of the features is applied prior to classification.
If any feature was incalculable, meaning it obtained a value of ∞, it was set equal to 0. The
features and subjects where this applies can be found in Table 16 of Appendix A.5.

3.3
Classification
Models and hyper-parameters
Support Vector Classifiers (SVC) and Logistic Regression (LogReg) were used to classify the data
based on the selected features. Prior to classification the labels PD and HC were label encoded
such that PD=1 and HC=0. The scoring parameter for all steps including hyper-parameter
tuning, feature selection and model evaluation was selected to be the f1-score, calculated as:
f1 = 2 · precision · recall
precision + recall
precision =
TP
TP + FP
recall =
TP
TP + FN
where precision is equal to the number of true positive (TP) results divided by the total number
of positive labels (the fraction of positives correctly labelled) and recall equals the number of
true positives (TP) divided by the number of labels that were actually supposed to be classified
as positives (the fraction of correctly classified positives (TP) over misclassified negatives (false
negatives, FN)). The f1-score is a harmonic weighted mean of the precision and recall, and its
values ranges from 0 to 1, with 1 being the best.[73]
The tested hyper-parameters for the LogReg and SVC can be seen in Table 4 and Table 5
respectively.

All kernel types use the regularization parameter C. The lower the regularization parameter,

and γ were based on the advised exponential grid search of these parameters.[71]

Splitting of Data
Initially all data (Dall) is randomly shuffled and split into 10 outer folds for model evaluation,
each fold consisting of Dtest(outer) and Dtrain(outer). The Dtrain(outer) data of each outer fold
is then split into 9 inner folds for model selection, each fold consisting of Dtest(inner) and
Dtrain(inner). Figure 10 shows the setup for the inner and outer folds for cross-validation, also
referred to as nested cross-validation.
Figure 10: Diagram of nested cross-validation using 10 outer folds and 9 inner folds, meaning
the test set in the outer and inner folds (Dtest(outer) and Dtest(inner)) are of the same size.
Each the outer and inner folds were generated by stratifying according to label (PD or HC),
meaning that the ratio of PD and HC is sought to be same in both the test set and train set.
Since features are extracted for each 1 minute of EEG data and the total measuring time being
3 minutes there exists 3 data points for each subject. These data points from the same sub-
ject must therefore be grouped to avoid data leakage when splitting data into test and train.
The splits were therefore also generated based on grouping of the subject ID such that all data
from a single subject belongs to either the test or train set, meaning a subject can not be both
trained and tested on within the same fold. This is done to insure that the test data is indeed
independent of the train data.
29 of 135
==PAGE:38==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
The purpose of the outer folds is model evaluation through estimation of the generalization of
the model while the inner folds are for model selection including hyper-parameter tuning and
feature selection.
The processing steps performed in the inner folds are for each tested classifier type as follows:
• Feature scaling of the features in Dtrain(inner) and Dtest(inner) of each fold, based on the
mean and standard deviation of the features in Dtrain(inner).
• Cross-validated hyper-parameter tuning using grid search of all parameters. Across
all inner folds the best model was selected as the one having the highest mean test score,
using f1-score as the scoring method.
• Feature selection was performed using the parameters of the previously selected best
model and cross-validated across the exact same inner folds as used for the hyper-parameter
tuning. For the LogReg and SVC using the ’linear’ kernel, Recursive Feature Elimination
(RFE) was performed, which provides the ranking of each feature. The SVC with ’rbf’
kernel instead used Sequential Feature Selection (SFS). The Dtrain(inner) data within each
inner fold was then updated by removing all of the eliminated features.
• Cross-validated hyper-parameter tuning was then again performed on the new data
set of Dtrain(inner) which consists of a subset of features. Cross-validation was performed
using exactly the same inner folds as previously. The best model was again selected as the
one having the highest mean f1-score averaged across all inner folds.
The processing of the inner folds was repeated for each of the outer folds, acquiring mean f1-
scores for each outer fold and classifier type. The mean f1-score and parameters of the best
model in each outer fold are provided in Section 4 Table 11 for each classifier type.
Feature Scaling
Standard scaling was performed for each individual feature such that new feature values (xnew)
are calculated as:
xnew = x −µ
σ
(19)
where x is the old feature value, µ and σ are the mean and standard deviation respectively for
the feature values of all data points in Dtrain(inner). Therefore the test set is scaled based on
the mean and standard deviation of the training data to avoid data leakage.
Hyper-parameter Tuning
The tuning of hyper-parameters was performed in the inner folds, by means of cross-validated
grid search using the GridSearchCV function with f1-score as the scoring method. The applica-
tion of grid search ensures that all combinations of hyper-parameters are tested.
Feature Selection
Features are removed from the dataset to improve performance and reduce computational cost
by reducing the dimensionality of the data. It was deemed impossible within the time frame to
test all combinations of features, as the feature space is rather large and the computational cost
would be to high. Elimination of features was performed using either cross-validated Recursive
Feature Elimination (RFE) or cross-validated Sequential Feature Selection (SFS), both using
f1-score as the scoring method. The feature elimination was cross-validated across the same
inner folds as the hyper-parameter tuning.
RFE needs the model to generate feature weights or coefficients, limiting its use to only be ap-
plicable for the LogReg and SVC with ’linear’ kernel. For RFE both the step size and minimum
30 of 135
==PAGE:39==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
number of features selected were both set equal to 1. The RFE starts with all features and then
iteratively removes the feature with the lowest model weight, each time evaluating the f1-score
and fitting the model on the new subset of features. Using RFE with cross-validation (RFECV)
the algorithm also determines the number of features to select.
For SVC with ’rbf’ kernel SFS was applied using the ’forward’ method, setting the number of
features to select equal to 30. This method does not need individual feature weights, as the
full feature set is evaluated, and then leaving one feature out at a time and evaluating which
omitted feature yielded the highest increase in f1-score. This process is performed iteratively
until the intended number of features is reached.
The Best Model
For each classifier type the best model was defined by the feature subspace of the most selected
features across the outer fold. The number of features selected for each model was defined as
all features selected by M or more models. M being an integer between 1 and 10, for which
is applies that M is the highest number of models a feature has been included in, that yields
greater than 5 total selected features. Since the regularization parameter relates to the number
of features in the feature space, the hyper-parameters are cross-validated over the outer folds
for the reduced features space. The best models are defined in Table 12.
Feature Importance
The features selected for each model of the outer folds are evaluated based on the distribution
of features within the categories of:
• Frequency band: delta, theta, alpha, beta, gamma and all bands
• Spatial level: graph, node and region
• Feature type: density, degree, clustering coefficient, betweenness centrality, closeness
centrality, current flow betweenness centrality, current flow closeness centrality, closeness
vitality, constraint, effective size, eccentricity, edges within, edges between, modularity,
s-metric, efficiency, sigma, omega, wiener index, average shortest path length, radius and
diameter
Statistical Analysis
Based on the obtained mean f1-scores of the individual classifier types, the model performance
was evaluated using resampling in the form of random permutation testing. Permutation was
performed using the features selected for the best models.
The process of permutation was implemented such that the labels of the entire dataset were
randomly shuffled, whilst maintaining the same label for each of the three observations (epochs)
for each subject. The data was then split into the outer fold, while stratifying for both label and
group (subject) in each split. Thereby the splitting of data into 10 folds such that no subject
was present in both a train and a test set to avoid data leakage and the labels were sought
balanced as much as possible between the test and train set.
Within each outer fold the hyper-parameters were tuned as described earlier, training data was
fitted to the best performing model and predictions were made using the corresponding test
data resulting in an f1-score for the corresponding outer fold.
The average f1-score was then calculated across all outer folds for each classifier type, the results
of which are seen in Table 12.
This process was repeated 10.000 times to estimate the null-distribution of the average f1-scores
of the models, as seen in Figure 17 and to obtain a p-value for the model performance, the
results of which can be seen in Table 12.

4.4
Permutation testing
Based on the models in Table 11 the best model of LogReg and SVC ’linear’ were defined by the
feature subspace of the most selected features across the outer fold, with the number of features
for each model being as defined in Table 12.
The average f1-scores in Table 12 were cross-validated across the outer folds, for which hyper-
parameter tuning was also applied.
Based on 10.000 permutations only the p-value of the
LogReg density-based model was below 0.05 %. This indicates a low probability of acquiring
an f1-score through random shuffling of the labels that is higher than the LogReg density-based
average f1-score of 0.726, as can also be visually estimated from Figures 17a and 17b. This is
subsequently also the model that had the highest average f1-score of the best models in Table 12
yet the lowest average f1-score of the original models in Table 11.
