5
Discussion
Thresholding
Based on the individually optimized threshold values in Tables 6 and 8 the combination of fre-
quency bands does not increase performance notably, yet the lack of improvement could be due
to the increase in the size of the feature space long with a fixed C parameter.
For the individually optimized weight-based threshold values in Table 6 there seems to be little
to no agreement on optimal threshold value across frequency bands or spatial levels, suggesting
that it might be beneficial to also threshold the spatial levels individually if multiple spatial lev-
els are intended to be used for future classification. This of course would add to the complexity
of the model, and could make it harder to interpret the results in terms of feature importance.
The density-based threshold in Table 8 have a greater overlap, which could be due to fewer
threshold values were tested.
Optimizing for all combinations of threshold values, see Tables 7 and 9, resulted in increased
f1-scores for all spatial levels, suggesting that if multiple frequency bands are to be included the
threshold values should be optimized across all frequency bands combined. This in turn meant
that most of the optimal threshold values differed between optimizing individual or combined
frequency bands.
In Table 12 the optimal threshold values of the combined feature space of graph+node+region
were mostly minimum and maximum threshold values, suggesting that optimal performance of
the features can be obtained by increasing graph sparsity in some frequency bands and decreas-
ing it in others, as was also evident from the violin plots in Figures 22 and 23 of Appendix A.6.
Similar findings for different feature types were reported by Philips et al. 2015 [64], where op-
timal range of graph sparsity for measuring the difference in Pearson correlation between AD
dementia groups and HC were seen to differ between different graph metrics. The paper by
Adamovich et al. 2022 [1] found significant changes in global connectivity measures (equal to
some of the graph based features tested in this thesis) as a function of threshold, further under-
lining the high impact of graph thresholding on the final results of a paper. The entirety of the
thesis illustrates that the choice of threshold method and value is non-trivial when taking both
frequency bands, models and feature types into account.
For this thesis fully connected and disconnected graph were avoided by incorporating constraints
into determining the threshold ranges, as described in Section 2.5. Fully connected graphs were
avoided since binarized fully connected graphs are indistinguishable from each other. Discon-
nected graphs on the other hand prevent the calculation of multiple features derived from path
lengths, since just a single disconnected node in the graph results in multiple node- and graph-
level features being incalculable, zero or infinite (see Table 17).
Based on the result in Figure 14 the most frequently selected features for both weight- and
density-based thresholding includes degree, closeness vitality and effective size, which are among
the ones affected by disconnected graphs. If a similar setup including these three features is to be
used in the future it would not be recommended to extend the threshold value range to higher
values than defined by Equation (17), as this would result in increased sparsity, introducing
disconnected graphs.
Alternatively methods should be incorporated to handle disconnected
graphs and nodes. The current minimum and maximum threshold values are calculated based
on the connectivity values of all subjects, meaning the ranges are likely to differ when applied to
other data sets and the span of the threshold ranges are expected to grow smaller for increasing
sizes of data sets.
46 of 135
==PAGE:55==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
Model performance
The models in Table 11 have a large variation in f1-scores and number of selected features of the
outer folds, resulting in average f1-scores around 0.5 with the best performing classifier being
the density-based SVC ’linear’. Some of the worst performing outer fold appear to be consistent
across the different classifiers, suggesting that the test data in outer fold 2 and 7 might differ
substantially from the corresponding training data.
The poor average performance could be due to the limitation in data size, yet other papers have
with the same data set managed to obtain accuracies of 96.13 [37], 98, 99.7 and 99.9 [4] when
comparing PD off medication vs. HC using either artificial neural networks, convolutional neural
networks, k-nearest neighbours or least-squares SVM.[74] These classifications were not based
on connectivity measures but mostly spectral decomposition, power and statistical measures,
indicating that PD and HC can be sufficiently separated using the San Diego data set. The
lower performance scores of Table 11 might therefore be due to the selected feature space or
non-optimal processing of thresholding, hyper-parameters feature selection or classifiers.
Feature Importance
There was found no tendencies of selecting features from specific frequency bands, which was
as expected when including multiple features, since AD and PD literature reports significant
differences for various features and frequency bands, see Appendix A.8 Tables 27 and 28.
Similarly none of the spatial levels tends to be over-represented in the models of Figure 12,
for which the graph level features were expected to be included to a higher degree based on
the over-representation of graph level analysis as opposed to node level in the literature, see
Appendix A.8 Tables 27 and 28. It was also expected that when not limiting the number of
features, the node level features, containing more detailed information about the graph, would
be able to represent the underlying brain network to a greater extend than the averaged out
features of both the region and graph level features. The more simple features of the graph
and region level where included based on other scientific findings from Tables 27 and 28 and
to possibly asses the usability of simplified features in combination with simpler classifier models.
Out of the 22 general feature types in Figure 13, degree, closeness vitality and effective size were
the most frequently selected. Despite the low performance of the models in Table 11 the three
feature types are still thought to reflect the differences between PD and HC that are measur-
able in the San Diego data set. The best models consisting of the most selected features were
comprised mainly of these three feature types, as seen in Figure 14.
The combination of degree, closeness vitality and effective size yields a measure of both the
integration, importance and redundancy of a node, which captures different aspects of the brain
network structure and possible change in length of signal pathways as a result of damages.
In Figure 16 the selected features of the best LogReg models were mapped onto their node
position, revealing a tendency for pairing of the degree and closeness vitality for the same node
position and frequency band. This could suggest a feature pair suitable for capturing a specific
characteristic of the brain network. The node pairs were mainly located in the frontal to central
region, which is in line with the finding of Aljalal et al. 2022 [4], who also based on the San
Diego data set the found most informative features to be positioned in the frontal area. Similarly
an AD study by Mehraram et al. 2020 [45] found reduced connectivity in the frontal region.
Hassan et al. 2017 [33] found functional connectivity to decrease as PD progresses, with loss
of connectivity between the frontal and temporal region thought to be an indicator of mild- to
moderate cognitive impairment for PD patients.
47 of 135
==PAGE:56==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
The paper by Mehraram et al. 2020 [45] found significant differences in global degree when
comparing PD to PD-Dementia (PD-D) and PD-D to AD. Hassan et al. 2017 [33] found no
significant differences between PD-CN, PD-MCI and PD-SCI using graph or node-to-node based
features, and postulated that the normalized features used (clustering coefficient, path length,
strength and global efficiency) might not be sensitive enough for detecting the reorganization
of the brain networks of PD patients with varying degrees of cognitive impairment, and that
more advanced graph and node based might detect these alterations. The features presented
in the thesis are thought to be of higher complexity and its findings are in line with those of
Hassan et al., being that more simple features might not be adequate for capturing the complex
brain network modifications thought to proceed during progression of neurological diseases and
cognitive impairment.
Papers reporting scientific findings for PD patients using closeness vitality and effective size
have not yet been identified. Additionally all of the graph theory derived features reported in
Table 28 of Appendix A.8 are on a graph level or averages of specific node positions or node
pairs. No papers have been identified that includes features to the extent covered in this thesis,
being that most PD graph theory papers mainly include path length and clustering coefficient.
Many papers provide results based on significance between PD and HC for individual graph
based features within each frequency band at given thresholds, and therefore does not include
the possible tendencies across frequency bands or features. Post-hoc evaluation of violin plots
of the most frequently used features in this thesis showed that there were some slight differences
in the distribution of PD and HC, yet none of them showed signs of possible application for
univariate classification. The workings of the brain is quite complex, and most node features or
graph averages thereof will struggle to capture all of the characteristics of the network even in
a simplified form of only 32 node positions.
Permutation Testing
For the best models in Table 12 the only tested model with a p-value below 0.05 for the per-
mutation testing was the density-based LogReg best model that reached an average f1-score of
0.726. This suggest the possibilities of acquiring sufficient validation performance on a limited
feature space of only 36 features, all from the three best feature types.
When limiting the feature space to the most frequently selected features across the outer folds,
they are selected based on classifiers that together have seen the entire data set. The average
f1-scores in Table 12 might therefore reflect overfitted models since the f1-scores could not be
acquired for unseen data in order to asses the generalization of the models. The best models
were included to reflect the possibilities within the San Diego data to distinguish the PD and
HC classes, and to asses which features might be best for this specific classification problem.
Apart from not being able to asses the generalization of the model within the same data set,
since all data has been used, the models are also only trained on data acquired from a single
EEG device for a small set of subject representing only HC and a single neurological disease,
namely PD. If a better generalization is preferred, then additional data sets should be included
to better capture the variation of neurological diseases, age, dementia stages and EEG devices
along with other factors.
The classifiers trained on the San Diego data set are likely to capture the effects of PD on the
human brain network, for which it can not be stated to what degree cognitive impairment and
dementia is reflected in the data or models. With PD patients on average developing dementia
48 of 135
==PAGE:57==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
10 years after their PD diagnosis, and mild-, moderate- and severe cognitive impairment likely
preceding the dementia diagnosis, some of the included patients might already be experiencing
cognitive symptoms despite the MMSE scores not suggesting a significant difference in cognitive
function between PD and HC. A study by Mamikonyan et al. 2009 [43] even found that 1/3
of PD patients with a normal MMSE score had a mild cognitive impairment when evaluated
using other cognitive impairment test including Hopkins Verbal Learning Test (memory), Stroop
Color-Word Test (executive function) and Tower of London-Drexel, indicating that early cogni-
tive impairment in PD patients can be missed when only assessing the MMSE score.
To better capture the cognitive impairment aspect of dementia, it should be sought to acquire
data that better captures the spectrum of cognitive impairment along with a wider range of
cognitive function scoring methods.
Reflections on project plan, goals and learning objectives
Graph thresholding was underestimated in terms of both computational time and possible ef-
fects on results. Based on findings of Adamovich et al. 2022 [1] it was decided to also include
threshold optimization in stead of randomly selecting threshold values. Optimizing across com-
binations of possible threshold values for all frequency bands yielded high computational costs,
with total time for calculating optimal thresholds for weight- and density-based thresholding
exceeding a week for each tested spatial level. This greatly exceeded the estimated duration of
signal processing, see Gantt chart in Appendix A.1. The individual two weeks of signal process-
ing were indented for the processing of AD data, which had to be postponed and lastly omitted
from the thesis due to time constrains, causing the 6. learning objective to not be met.
Additionally general computational time was also underestimated, since some classifiers took
too much time to be include within the provided time frame, given the limitations on process-
ing power. Initially the polynomial kernel was also intended to be included as one of the SVC
models, but was cut do to very high computational time. The remaining parts of the Gantt
chart were followed by writing theory and methods as each computational step was implemented.
The overall project goal was constructed based on the inclusion of both PD and AD, which with
AD not included prohibits the comparison of the two disease models.
Future work
The entirety of this thesis was based on the considerations of both AD and PD, as it was
intended to also include comparisons between the two diseases. This thesis functions as the pre-
liminary work into characterizing neurological diseases using machine learning and graph theory.
The NIA-AA research framework [34] puts emphasis on the Alzheimer’s and cognitive contin-
uum, suggesting that regression as opposed to classification might better capture the continuous
change of AD patients, applicable to all neurological diseases causing cognitive impairment. The
use of other connectivity measures and features could be included in futures studies, along with
data of a wider range of neurological diseases and severity of cognitive impairment.
The current SanDiego data is measured during for eo-re-EEG, a less used condition compared to
ec [46], which is also confirmed by the majority of the papers included in Tables 29 and 30 being
for the ec-rsEEG condition. Acquire ec-rs-EEG data, would be of interest since the ec condition
has been said to be more sensitive when assessing brain activity [46] and is also recommended
by Babiloni 2021 [10] for measuring AD patients.
49 of 135
==PAGE:58==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
6
Conclusion
This thesis constitutes the preliminary work on evaluating the usability of graph theory metrics
for characterizing brain network dysfunction of neurocognitive disorders. Logistic Regression
and Support Vector Classifiers were used to classify Parkinson’s Disease patients from healthy
controls using graph theory metrics based on Imaginary Coherence of resting-state EEG as a
measure of connectivity between brain regions.
The effect of graph thresholding was investigated, for which it was found that optimal threshold
value varied greatly between frequency bands and spatial level, with neither weight-based or
density-based thresholding consistently performing better than the other. This suggests that
choice of method and optimization should depend on the specific use case, as non optimal thresh-
old could reduce differences between the groups sought to be differentiated.
For classification of PD and HC no preference for the models to select features of specific fre-
quency bands or spatial level was found. The feature types of most importance were found to
be node degree, closeness vitality and effective size, with their overwhelming presence in the
selected features across all tested models indicating the possible usage of these three features
for network based classification of PD in future studies. A simplified model of the best features
reached an f1-score of 0.726 for a logistic regression model using density-based thresholding and
only 36 total features, however the generalization is undetermined and the model could therefore
be overfitted. The model’s selection of features reflects patterns of high estimated importance
of node degree and closeness vitality within the same frequency band and node position in the
frontal region and effective size mainly in the left occipital region.
Theses results are based on small data set of a single type of NCD reflecting only mild cognitive
impairment, and the generalizability of the proposed features of importance and graph theory in
general must be evaluated across a broader spectrum of cognitive impairment before is usability
can be fully evaluated.
It is therefore proposed that future studies should asses if the findings of degree, closeness vitality
and effective size importance can be replicated for other data sets, and further investigate the
ability for graph theory based classification to generalize across different neurological disorders
and degrees of cognitive impairment, with thoughtful consideration for the importance of the
thresholding process.

A
Appendix
A.1
Project Plan
Project description
Parkinson’s Disease (PD) is a neurodegenerative disease and a prevalent cause of dementia. It
shares neuronal networks characteristics with Alzheimer’s Disease (AD), another common cause
of dementia. PD and AD share pathological changes of the default mode network compared
to healthy controls [80, 69, 25] in Functional magnetic resonance imaging, which indicate an
alteration of the flow of information between regions of the brain. In electroencephalography
(EEG) studies, PD patients show an increase in the lower- and a decrease in the higher frequency
bands [75], similar to that of AD [35]. Several multivariate discriminatory models have been
employed to distinguish PD patients from healthy controls (HCs) using resting state (rs-)EEG
data.[74, 3, 38]
This project will attempt to employ state-of-the-art machine learning methods for discovering
characteristic brain network changes in rs-EEG that differentiate PD and HC cohorts along
with AD and HC. The models will subsequently be tested on the opposite dataset, such that
the model trained on PD and HC data is tested on AD and HC data and vice versa, to fur-
ther investigate differences and similarities between the two diseases.
From the rs-EEG the
Imaginary Coherence (ImCoh) is used as a measure of brain connectivity between each EEG
electrode pair. A graph is constructed based on the thresholded connectivity measures, where
multiple thresholding techniques will be tested due to the difficulty of obtaining comparable
thresholded graphs across multiple subjects. Simple graph characteristic features like degree,
clustering coefficient, characteristic path length etc. to be used for classification are extracted
for multiple frequency bands. The classification model should be easy to interpret and therefore
includes training of both Support Vector Classification (SVC) and Logistic Regression (LogReg)
Classification with regularization on number of features in the model and with Recursive Fea-
ture Elimination (RFE) to evaluate feature importance. Model optimization is performed using
nested cross-validation (CV) for hyper-parameter tuning.
The dataset with 15 PD and 16 HC subjects is obtained from the open source OpenNeuro
database (accession number ds002778). Prior to project admission it was planned to use the
dataset with 10 AD and 5 HC subjects obtained from the ALZLIGHT Pilot study (ClinicalTri-
als.gov Identifier: NCT04574921) through existing collaborations between Zealand University
Hospital and DTU Compute, but in the meantime access was also granted to a larger dataset of
230 subjects split into groups of 33 Healthy Controls Elders (60+ years old), 34 Subjective Cog-
nitive Decline, 79 Mild Cognitive Impairment, 48 AD and 36 Healthy Young (25-40 years old).
The larger AD dataset was acquired through signing of a non-disclosure agreement, samples of
the data can be acquired from zenodo.org (identifier: 4316608).
58 of 135
==PAGE:67==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
Learning objectives
1. Understand electrophysiology of EEG
2. Understand and implement pre-processing of EEG data
3. Extract relevant physiological features from EEG data
4. Train Classifier to distinguish between PD and HC groups
5. Evaluate performance of classification using cross-validation and resampling
6. Use developed methods transferred to AD data and investigate similarities of groups
Project goal
To construct classifiers for distinguishing between HC subjects and PD patients along with HC
subjects and AD patients, and then later test the classifiers with data from the opposite disease
dataset. The classification should be based on graph network features extracted from measures
of EEG based connectivity, and the importance of each feature should be evaluated for both
diseases.

A.2
Removal of ICA components, EEG channels and EEG time segments
The removal of ICA components, EEG channels and time segments through visual inspection
is determined based the following criteria, examples of which can be seen in Figure 20. Firstly
EEG channels are marked for removal, secondly time segments are marked for removal, thirdly
ICA components are marked for removal and lastly the EEG time segments to be removed are
double checked after removal of the selected ICA components. As proposed by Cohen [20], for
the purpose of reproducibility the removed ICA components, EEG channels and time segments
for each subject are listed in Table 13.
Criteria for removal of an EEG channel are:
• Channel is deemed noisy for more than half of the duration of the EEG measurement time.
See example in Figure 18a.
• High power over all frequencies relative to all other channels.
See example in Figure 18b.
Criteria for removal of an EEG time segment are:
• EEG channels are visually estimated to be highly affected by noise other than eye blinks.
See example in Figure 19.
Criteria for removal of an ICA eye-blink component are:
• Temporal element similar to that of an approximate blinking length and rate.
See example in the first row of Figure 20b.
• Periodic and non-noise like temporal occurrence of ’eye-blinks’ in the segment image.
See example in the top right of Figure 20c.
• High weighting of the electrodes in the prefrontal region (Fp1, Fp2, AF3, AF4).
See example of ICA component ICA000 in Figure 20a.
• Non-noise like smooth decaying frequency spectrum, with presence of lower frequencies.
See example in the top left of Figure 20c.

A.5
Network Features Overview
List of network feature formula for un-weighted and un-directed graphs omitting self-loops. The
included features are a non-exhaustive selection of both graph-based, node-based and region-
based features.
For all formulas it applies that:
A
= adjacency matrix (symmetric for undirected graphs)
com
= communities of nodes
G
= graph
Ge(n)
ego graph of a node
Gl
= equivalent lattice graph
Gr
= equivalent random graph
N
= total number of nodes in graph
n
= node
reg
= region of nodes
General
Number of possible edges
The maximum number of edges in an undirected graph (G) that has a total of N nodes.
mmax(G) = 1
2N(N −1)
(20)
Number of edges
The total number of edges in a graph.
m(G) = 1
2
N
X
i,j=1
Aij
(21)
where Aij is the value of the adjacency matrix corresponding to the edge between node i
and node j. For an unweighted (binary) graph the value of Aij is 1 if an edge connects
node i and j and 0 otherwise. The sum of values of the adjacency matrix is divided by 2
as un-directed adjacency matrices are symmetric, thereby each edge is represented twice
at both location Aij and Aji
Range: [0-mmax]
Density
The density (D) of a graph is a measure of how connected a graph is, defined as the
fraction of edges present in the graph out of all possible edges.
D(G) = 2 · m(G)
N(N −1) =
m(G)
mmax(G)
(22)
where m(G) is the number of edges in the graph and mmax(G) is the number of possible
edges in the graph.
Range: [0:1]
A density of 0 means that the graph has no edges (fully disconnected) and a density of 1
means that the graph has the maximum number of edges (fully connected). Any number
between 0 and 1 reflects the ratio of edges that exists in the graph out of all possible edges.

Degree
Node degree
[40]
The number of edges connected to node i, also equal to the number of neighbouring nodes
to node i. Node degree is also referred to as local degree.
k(ni) =
N
X
j=1
Aij
(23)
Range: [0:N-1]
A node degree of 0 means that the node is not connected to any other nodes, and the
graph is therefore disconnected. The distribution of node degrees for the entire graph can
also be used to describe the network.[68]
Linear dependence with: degree centrality
Global degree
[40]
The average number of edges connected to a node in the graph (G).
k(G) = 1
N ·
N
X
i=1
k(ni) = 2 · m(G)
N
(24)
where k(ni) is the node degree and m(G) is the number of edges in the graph.
Range: [0:N-1]
A value of 0 indicates the graph is fully disconnected and N-1 that it is fully connected.
Clustering Coefficient
The clustering coefficient is the degree to which a node is a part of a cluster, while the av-
erage clustering coefficient is the tendency for nodes to form clusters within the graph.[10]
Clustering Coefficient
[78]
The clustering coefficient (C) of a node is a measure of how clustered the node and its
neighbouring surroundings are.
C(ni) =
Number of edges between ni’s neighboring nodes
Number of possible edges between ni’s neighboring nodes
(25)
Range: [0:1]
The higher the value the more clustered the node is. Clustering coefficient can be inter-
preted as a measure of network durability, such that high values indicate that if a node
was lost at that location there is a high likelihood that the neighbouring nodes would still
be connected and thereby to some degree preserve the function of the affected area.[78]
Avg. Clustering Coefficient
[40]
The average clustering coefficient is also referred to as the global clustering coefficient and
is simply the average clustering coefficient of all nodes in the graph.
C(G) = 1
N ·
N
X
i=1
C(ni)
Range: [0:1]
The higher the value the more clustered the graph is on average. A value of 1 would
indicate a fully connected graph [77] and 0 a fully disconnected graph.

Katrine Dam Christensen

Technical University of Denmark
Path Length
[78]
A path length is the distance between two nodes calculated as the number of edges on the
path between them, and is defined as:
|path(ni, nj)|
(26)
where path(ni, nj) is a all possible paths between node i and j while |·| denotes the length
of the path.
Range: [1:N-1]
For calculating the path length between all pairs of nodes, it is assumed that the graph
is not disconnected, meaning that there exists a path between all pairs of nodes. If the
graph is disconnected, non-existing paths are commonly given an infinite path length [78].
A path length of N-1 is only possible if all nodes are arranged in succession with node i
and j being at each end.
Shortest Path Length
[40, 78]
The minimum number of edges needed to form a path between node i and node j, and
thereby the shortest path between them is defined as:
S(ni, nj) =
N
min
i,j=1, i̸=j |path(ni, nj)|
(27)
Range: [1:N-1]
A value of 1 indicates that node i and j are directly connected to each other and are
thereby neighbouring nodes. A shortest path length of N-1 is only possible if all nodes are
arranged in succession with node i and j being at each end.
Average Shortest Path Length
[40, 78]
Also referred to as: Characteristic Path Length
The average shortest path length of all pairs of nodes. It describes how well connected
the graph is and can be seen as a measure of how easily information can be distributed to
other areas of the network.[78]
L(G) =
1
N · (N −1) ·
N
X
i,j=1, i̸=j
S(ni, nj)
(28)
Range: [0:1]
An average shortest path length of 0 means that the graph is fully disconnected and a
value of 1 means that it is fully connected. The summation is averaged by dividing by
N · (N −1), being the total number of edges in the graph when omitting self-loops.
Linear dependence with: wiener index
Wiener Index
Also referred to as: Sum of Shortest Path Lengths
The sum of all shortest path lengths between all pairs of nodes.
W(G) =
N
X
i,j=1, i̸=j
S(ni, nj)
(29)
Range: [0:N(N-1)]

==PAGE:77==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
If the sum of all shortest paths is equal to 0 the graph is fully disconnected and N ·(N −1)
if the graph is fully connected since the total number of possible shortest paths (omitting
self-loops when i ̸= j) is N · (N −1). If the graph is disconnected the Wiener Index would
be infinite.
Linear dependence with: average shortest path length
Eccentricity
The length of the longest of the shortest path connecting node i to all other nodes.
Ecc(ni) =
N
max
j=1 S(ni, nj)
(30)
Range: [0:N-1]
A value of 0 meaning that the graph is fully disconnected. A longest shortest path length
of N-1 is only possible if all nodes are arranged in succession with node i being at one of
the ends.
A node is referred to as the central node is the node that has eccentricity equal to the
radius of the graph. The absolute centre of a graph is the node with the lowest eccentricity.
Radius
The length of the shortest of the longest shortest path between all pairs of nodes, which
is the same as minimum eccentricity.
Rad(G) =
N

The length of the longest of the longest shortest path between all pairs of nodes, which is
the same as maximum eccentricity.

Efficiency of a path
[40]
The efficiency of a pair of nodes (node i and node j) is the inverse of the shortest path
length between them.
Eff(ni, nj) =
1
S(ni, nj)
(33)
Range: [
1
N−1:∞]
An efficiency of
1
N−1 is possible only if all nodes are arranged in succession with node i and
j being at either end, being a case of ’worst’ efficiency in exchange of information between
node i and node j. An efficiency of ∞being if the two nodes can not be connected by any
path. The ’best’ exchange of information is obtained for the efficiency value of 1, when the
path between two nodes is equal to 1 and they are therefore directly connected making
them neighbouring node.
Efficiency of a path is therefore simply a transformation of shortest path length.

Global Efficiency
[5]
The global efficiency is the average efficiency of all pairs of nodes and is used to quantify
the efficiency by which information is exchanged within the graph network.

The global efficiency of a graph is the normalized Harary Index, which is the sum of the
reciprocal of distances between all pairs of nodes.[5]

[56]
Local efficiency of a node i is equal to the average global efficiency of a subgraph Gsub
consisting of node i and all of its neighbouring nodes (the nodes directly connected to

calculated over all nodes in the graph.

Small-world
Small-worldness covers the combination of high local connectivity and hubs connecting
distant nodes from different clusters.[10]
Small-worlds networks are characterized by high clustering coefficient and low average
shortest path length, which is based on optimized state of minimizing resource cost and
maximizing the flow of information between the components of the network.
Small-world index (sigma)
[78]
The ratio of global clustering coefficient compared to that of an equivalent random graph
(Gr) over the ratio of average shortest path length compared to that of an equivalent
random graph.
SW σ(G) = C(G)/ ¯C(Gr)
L(G)/¯L(Gr)
(36)
where C(G) and L(G) are the global clustering coefficient and average shortest path length
respectively.
¯C(Gr) and ¯L(Gr) are the global clustering coefficient and average shortest
path length calculated as the average over a fixed number (R) of equivalent random graphs
(Gr), for Gr = [Gr
1, Gr
2, ..., Gr
R] defined as:
¯C(Gr) = 1
R
R

Katrine Dam Christensen

R

i=1
L(Gr
i )
where Gr
i is the i’th random graph, each random graph being based on the nodes and
edges of the original graph (G) and R being the total number of realizations of a random
graph. A random graph is constructed by randomly swapping the edges of the original
graph, while the resulting random graph is still connected.[78]
It is therefore a measure of how similar the graph (G) is to a random graph (Gr). Therefore
the more clustered the nodes and the shorter the paths between the nodes, the less random
the graph and the higher the value of sigma. A value of sigma above 1 means that the
graph can be classified as a small-world graph.
Small-world index (omega)
[78]
The ratio of the average shortest path length of an equivalent random graph (Gr) compared
to the graph (G) minus the ratio of the global clustering coefficient for the graph (G)
compared to an equivalent lattice graph (Gl).
SW ω(G) =

fixed number (R) of equivalent random graphs (Gr) and ¯C(Gl) is the global clustering
coefficient calculated as the average over a fixed number (Lt) of equivalent lattice graphs

the original graph (G), Lt is the total number of realizations of a lattice graph. A lattice
graph is constructed by latticizing the original graph (G) by swapping its edges.
The omega value is a measure of how similar the graph is to a random graph and a lattice
graph. Lattice graphs are characterized by their high values of both C and L while random
graphs have low values of both C and L.[78] Negative values means high similarity with a
lattice graph and positive values means high similarity with a random graph. Values close
to zero increases the small-world characteristics of the graph.

Degree Centrality
The fraction of nodes that node i is directly connected to.
degree centrality(ni) = k(ni)
N −1
(38)
Range: [0:1]
The degree centrality is simply the normalized node degree and the two features will
therefore have a linear correlation of 1.
Linear dependence with: node degree
Betweenness Centrality
71 of 135
==PAGE:80==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
The betweenness of a node i is the ratio of shortest paths that pass through the node.
betweenness centrality(ni) =
N
X
j,k=1, j̸=k̸=i
σ(nj, nk|ni)
σ(nj, nk)
(39)
where σ(nj, nk) is the number of shortest paths between node j and k and σ(nj, nk|ni)
are the number of shortest paths between node j and k that pass through node i.
The betweenness relates to the importance of the node in terms of propagation of informa-
tion between different brain regions. High values of betweenness are therefore associated
with high importance and thereby also could result in higher consequences for the function-
ing of the network if that specific node was lost.[78] Normalized betweenness centrality of
1 means that all shortest paths between all pairs of nodes pass through the single node, in-
dicating that the node is an important link between brain regions. Betweenness centrality
of 0 means that none of the shortest paths pass through the single node.
Closeness Centrality
Closeness Centrality is equal to N-1 times the reciprocal of the Average Shortest Path
Length calculated only over the shortest paths between node i and all other nodes in the
graph.
closeness centrality(ni) =
N −1
PN−1
j=1 S(ni, nj)
(40)
Range: [ 2(N−1)
N(N+1):1]
Higher values of closeness centrality indicates a higher degree of centrality of the node.
A value of 1 would indicate that the node is directly connected to all other nodes in the
graph, since the sum of shortest paths would be equal to N −1. If all nodes were placed
in succession with node i at one of the ends, the sum of shortest paths would be equal to
N(N + 1)/2 resulting in the lowest possible closeness centrality of
2(N−1)
N(N+1).
Current Flow Betweenness centrality
[14]
Also referred to as: Random-Walk Betweenness Centrality
For formula see the NetworkX current flow betweenness centrality algorithm.
Current Flow Closeness centrality
[14]
Also referred to as: Information Centrality
For formula see the NetworkX current flow closeness centrality algorithm.
Closeness Vitality
[14]
The closeness vitality of a node ni is the change in the sum of shortest path lengths for
all node pairs when omitting node ni. Since the sum of shortest path lengths is equal to
the Wiener Index, the closeness vitality can be calculated as:
closeness vitality(ni) = W(G) −W(G −ni)
(41)
where W(G) is the Wiener Index of the entire graph G and W(G−ni) is the Wiener Index
calculated for all nodes in graph G apart from node ni.
72 of 135
==PAGE:81==
Katrine Dam Christensen
02/07/2023
Technical University of Denmark
The closeness vitality of a node indicates the importance of the node in the graph, by
representing the change of path lengths between all pairs of nodes if node ni is removed
from the graph. Removing a node from the graph will mean that the Wiener index is
summed over one less node, and subsequently could reduce the sum, but if the node is of
high importance in graph (e.g. if many shortest paths pass through the node or if the node
is an articulation point where removing it would cause the graph to become disconnected)
the Wiener Index could increase, resulting in negative closeness vitality values. If the
graph becomes disconnected by removing ni the closeness vitality would be -∞.[14]
Mutual weight
The mutual weight (mw) of two node is equal to the sum of the edge weight in both
directions.
mw(ni, nj) = Aij + Aji
where A is the adjacency matrix such that Aij is edge weight from node i to node j and Aji
is edge weight from node j to node i. Given that the adjacency matrix of an undirected
graph is symmetric, the mutual weight between two nodes is simply mw(ni, nj) = 2·Aij. If
the graph is also binary the mw is 2 if the two nodes are directly connected (neighbouring
nodes) and 0 otherwise.
The normalized mutual weight (nmw) is calculated as:
nmw(ni, nj) =
mw(ni, nj)
P
nk∈Gn(ni) mw(ni, nk)
(42)
where Gn(ni) denotes the sub graph of neighbouring nodes of node i. For a binary un-
weighted graph the normalized mutual weight therefore equals
2
2·len(Gn(ni)) if node i and j
are neighbouring and 0 otherwise. The length (len) equals the number of nodes in a graph.
As the number of neighbouring nodes increases, the normalized mutual weight decreases.

Effective size
[13]
It is a measure of redundancy of a node based on the number of edges between its neigh-
bouring nodes.
effective size(n) = len(Gn(ni)) −2 · m(Gn(ni))
len(Gn(ni))
(45)
where Gn(ni) is the neighbour graph for node ni, a neighbour graph being a subgraph
of G containing only the neighbouring nodes of node ni (excluding ni from the subgraph
as well). The length (len) of the graph denotes the number of nodes in the graph and
m(Gn(Ni)) denotes the total number of edges in the neighbouring graph, as defined by
Equation (21).
Range: [1:m(Gn(ni))]
The higher the number of neighbouring nodes and the lower the number of edges be-
tween them, the higher the effective size. The effective size decreases with 2/len(G) per
additional edge between the neighbouring nodes. If there exists zero edges between the
neighbouring nodes, then the effective size of the node is equal to the number of neigh-
bouring nodes (or simply the number of nodes in the ego graph m(Gn(ni)) not including
node ni). When the number of edges between the neighbouring nodes is equal to the
maximum number of edges mmax(Gn(ni)) the effective size is equal to 1.
The 2·m(Gn(ni))
len(Gn(ni)) part of the equation is known as redundancy, which is also equal to the
average degree of the neighbouring nodes. Alternatively effective size can then also be
described as the size of the neighbouring graph (in number of nodes) minus the average
degree of the neighbouring nodes.
Modularity
[32]
The modularity (Q) of a binary undirected graph can be seen as a measure of how well the
graph has been divided into communities.[19] It reflects the degree to which a graph can be
subdivided into delineated groups of nodes (communities).[10] Modularity is defined as the
fraction of edges within a group or community (com) minus the fraction of edges within
the group or community if all edges in the graph were redistributed randomly. Positive
modularity therefore suggest that there are more edges within the group than expected if
the edges were distributed randomly.[32]
Q(G) =
1

is the probability that an edge exists between node i and j if connections between all
nodes of the graph were made at random while maintaining the same degree for both nodes.
Modularity will be high for graphs where the communities are based on good divisions of
the network, given that good divisions maximize the number of edges inside the commu-
nities and minimize the number of edges between communities. The closer the fraction of
edges that are inside the communities of the graph is similar to that of a random graph
the closer the modularity would be to zero. In general modularity values above 0.3 indi-
cates significant community structures within the graph.[19] Negative values occur if there
exists less edges within the community than the number of edges expected to be within
the community given the probability of the existence of an edge.
Range: [-1:1]

The graph-based features of Table 18 indicates that the delta band has the highest f1-scores
and is fairly stable across all threshold values, with all f1-scores being above 0.5, while the all
bands consistently has very low performance across all threshold values. All frequency bands
apart from delta all have f1-scores less than 0.5. For bands such as theta and alpha the choice of
threshold value has a higher impact on the f1-score. The f1-score of the theta and alpha band
tends to decrease with increasing threshold value, suggesting that graph based-features within
these frequency bands decreases in performance as the graphs get more sparse.
The node-based features in Table 19 have a higher f1-score than the graph-based features at all
threshold values and frequency bands apart from delta: th = [−0.06 : −0.01], beta: th = −0.02
and gamma: th = [−0.03 : −0.01].
The region-based features in Table 20 slightly outperforms the node-based features for most
threshold values, and therefore also outperforms the graph-based when assessing the optimal
threshold value f1-scores. The f1-score of 0.610 for the delta band at th = −0.08 being the only
performance above 0.6 for the weight-based thresholding of any spatial level and threshold value.
Between the three spatial levels, there is no overlap of which threshold value has the highest
performance, apart from beta: th = −0.02 that is the highest performing threshold value for
both the graph-based and region-based features.
The graph+node+region-based features combined in Table 21 overall does not have better
performance than the individual spatial levels.
The optimal threshold values of the combined spatial levels overlaps with the node-based opti-
mal thresholds for both the delta, theta and gamma band, and overlaps with the alpha and all
bands for the region-based and graph-based features respectively.

Table 25: F1-scores for different thresholds evaluated for each frequency band individually. Each
f1-score is based on the individual frequency bands using the joined graph+node+region-
based features and density-based thresholding. Highest f1-score for each frequency band is
written in bold font.
The graph-based features of Table 22 have highest performance for higher threshold values at
0.3 or 0.4, yet the none of the f1-score are above 0.5, suggesting overall poor performance of
density based thresholding for graph features on each frequency band individually. As opposed
to the weight-based threshold of graph features the performance here tends to increase rather
than decrease for increasing threshold values.
The node-based features in Table 23 have a higher f1-score than the graph-based features at
all threshold values and frequency bands. As opposed to the tendency for higher performance
at higher threshold values for the graph-based features the node-based features tends to have
higher performance at lower threshold values. The gamma and all bands are the only frequency
bands that for all threshold values does not exceed a performance of 0.5.
The region-based features in Table 24 to a higher degree outperforms the node-based features
for most threshold values than was the case for the weight-based thresholding. The region-based
features have a higher f1-score than the node-based features at all threshold values and frequency
bands apart from alpha: th = [0.3, 0.4], beta: th = 0.4 and gamma: th = [0.1 : 0.4]. Like the
node-based features the highest performance is generally seen at lower threshold values, the
opposite of which was seen for the graph-based features.
Between the three spatial levels there is a higher degree of overlap of which threshold value has
the highest performance than for the weight-based thresholding, as node-based and region-based
have an overlap of optimal thresholds for all frequency bands except beta and all bands.
The graph+node+region-based features combined in Table 25 does not have better perfor-
mance than the individual spatial levels, apart from th = 0.4 for both the alpha, beta and gamma
band. The alpha band at th = 0.4 being the only density-based thresholding performance above
0.6 when individually optimizing the frequency bands.
The optimal threshold values of the combined spatial levels overlaps with the graph-based op-
timal thresholds for all frequency bands except delta, and therefore shares the graph-based
tendencies for higher threshold values to perform better.

Technical University of Denmark
For the graph-based violin plots in Figure 22 generally have the highest f1-scores across all fre-
quency bands, with almost all f1-scores are above 0.5. All of the region-based violin plots have
a median f1-score above 0.5, while for the node-based on the other hand, only the gamma band
at th = −0.04 has a median value above 0.5. The distribution of the graph+node+region-
based violin plots for all frequency bands tends to be most similar to that of the region-based.
The change in f1-score as a function of threshold level for each of the different frequency bands
a spatial levels is described in the following:
• For the delta band the distribution of f1-scores of the node-based features tend to decrease
with increasing threshold value.
• For the theta band the distribution of f1-scores of the node-based features tend to decrease
with increasing threshold value.
• For the alpha band the distribution of f1-scores is seemingly not affected greatly by the
change in threshold value, with the only noticeable changes being an increased variance of
the graph and graph+node+region level.
• For the beta band the distribution of f1-scores across all spatial levels tends to decrease
only for the highest threshold value.
• For the gamma the distribution of f1-scores of the node-based features tends to decrease at
increasing threshold value while the graph-based tends to increase. The remaining levels
seem to only have slight changes in the distribution of f1-scores as a function of threshold
value.
• For the all bands the distribution of the f1-scores shifts downwards at higher threshold
values for all spatial levels.
In summary the all bands has the most consistent change in f1-score distribution as a function
of threshold value across all spatial levels, namely a decrease in f1-score for increasing threshold
value. The gamma band had both an increase and a decrease in f1-scores for increasing threshold
value, for the graph-based and node-based features respectively.

For the graph-based violin plots in Figure 23 only some median values are above 0.5, whereas
for the node-based all f1-scores are below 0.5 with median values around 0.4. For region-
based and graph+node+region-based most of the f1-scores are above 0.5.
The change in f1-score as a function of threshold level for each of the different frequency bands
and spatial levels is described in the following:
• For the delta band the distribution of f1-scores for the graph-based features seems to have
a local maximum performance threshold value while the remaining spatial levels appear
stable across all threshold values.
• For the theta band the distribution of f1-scores at all spatial levels appear stable across all
threshold values.
• For the alpha band the distribution of f1-scores for the region-based features tends to
decrease slightly at increasing threshold value while the remaining spatial levels appear
stable across all threshold values.
• For the beta band the distribution of f1-scores for the graph- and region-based features
seems to have a local maximum performance threshold value while the graph+node+region
features tend to increase with increasing threshold value.
• For the gamma band the distribution of f1-scores for the node- and region-based features
tends to decrease at increasing threshold value, while the graph-based tend to increase.
• For the all bands the distribution of f1-scores at all spatial levels appear stable across all
threshold values.
In summary at threshold values of 0.4 for the alpha band and 0.4 and 0.5 for the beta band are
the only times that the graph+node+region features noticeably outperforms all of the individual
spatial levels.
Similar to the weight-based violin plots in Figure 22, the graph+node+region level seems most
comparable to that of the region level, but for the beta band the combination of graph+node+region
even outperforms the region level at high threshold values.
A main difference in the findings from the weight-based and density-based distribution of f1-
scores is that for density-based thresholding the region-based features are often comparable to
or even better than the graph-based, the opposite of which was the case for the weight-based
f1-score distributions.

A.7
Model Performance of SVC ’rbf’
Table 26 includes the model performance of SVC ’rbf’ kernel with fixed number of features
at 30. The ’rbf’ kernel therefore didn’t have the same freedom in terms of method of feature
selection, which was due to the very high computation time using this specific classifier with
large feature space. The f1-scores indicates a distinctive difference between weight-based and
density-based SVC ’rbf’, with a significant difference between the average f1-scores (p-value ¡
0.05). It should be noted that the threshold values were optimized using a LogReg classifier,
and the performance in Table 26b does therefore not necessarily reflect optimal performance.

A.8
AD and PD findings in the literature
Tables 27 and 28 contain the EEG findings of AD and PD based on the papers listed in Tables 29
and 30 respectively.
Table 27 contains the EEG findings of AD patients. The arrows ↑and ↓indicates that the
feature is either increased or decreased for AD relative to HC. Red colored text indicates that
the feature was calculated based on a weighted graph as opposed to a binary graph.
Table 28 contains the EEG findings of PD patients of different stages of cognitive impairment.
The arrows ↑and ↓indicates that the feature is either increased or decreased for PD relative to
HC. If two classes are mentioned in brackets, e.g. MCI vs. CN, an increase would indicate that
the first mentioned class is greater than the latter class and vice versa. If nothing is mentioned
in the brackets then the comparison is between PD and HC, where an increase would mean that
PD is greater than HC. If only one class is mentioned then the comparison is between that class
and HC, where an increase would mean that the feature is higher for the stated class than for
HC. Red colored text indicates that the feature was calculated based on a weighted graph as
opposed to a binary graph.

A.9
Python Scripts
All processing and classification is performed in Python using packages of NetworkX [55], MNE [49], MNE-BIDS [48, 9] and MNE-connectivity [50].
Figure 24: Processing pipeline of the python script.
The Python scripts in the following sections includes: bidsify.py, inspect data.py, preprocessing.py, connectivity.py, feature extraction.py,
classification.py and util.py.

Katrine Dam Christensen

Technical University of Denmark
A.9.1
bidsify.py
The main reason for the data not being BIDS compliant was that ’gender’ was used instead of
’sex’ which caused issued for the MNE-bids functions.[48] It should be noted that additional
errors in the data files include:
• power line noise defined as 50 Hz instead of 60 Hz in file: data/source/sub-?/ses-?/eeg/sub-
? ses-? task-rest eeg.json for subject hc21, hc25, hc31 and hc32.
• data acquisition time had been overwritten due to preprocessing of subject pd6 and pd16.

A.9.4
connectivity.py
The connectivity is calculated using the spectral connectivity epochs function from the
mne connectivity library.
Using the MNE function with mode = ’multitaper’ long buffer-
zones at the ends of the segments are not necessary, which would not be the case if mode
= ’cwt morlet’ (continuous wavelet transform using Morlet wavelets) was used.[52] The MNE
defines ImCoh similar to Section 2.4 Equation (12).[52]

s u b j e c t s
within

defined

A.9.6
classification.py
For the classification script the variables THRESHOLD MODE is selected as either ’weight’
or ’density’ while MODEL is selected as either ’logreg’, ’svclinear’ or ’svcrbf’. The SVC using
polynomial kernel was also implemented but not used, but can be accessed by setting MODEL
equal to ’svcpoly’.
